<td colspan="2" class="noprint" style="text-align:center;background:#a1ffc3">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_phosphorus" title="Template:Infobox phosphorus"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li>

</div>
| <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a> | <a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q674" class="extiw" title="d:Q674">in Wikidata</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Phosphorus</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol&#160;<b>P</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>&#160;15. As an element, phosphorus exists in two major forms—<a href="/wiki/Yellow_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Yellow phosphorus">white phosphorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Red phosphorus">red phosphorus</a>—but because it is highly <a href="/wiki/Reactivity_(chemistry)" title="Reactivity (chemistry)">reactive</a>, phosphorus is never found as a free element on Earth. At 0.099%, phosphorus is the most abundant <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogen</a> in the Earth's crust. With few exceptions, minerals containing phosphorus are in the maximally oxidised state as inorganic <a href="/wiki/Phosphate_minerals" title="Phosphate minerals">phosphate rocks</a>.</p>
<p>The first form of elemental phosphorus to be produced (white phosphorus, in 1669) emits a faint glow when exposed to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> – hence the name, taken from Greek mythology, <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Φωσφόρος</span> meaning "light-bearer" (Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Lucifer" title="Lucifer">Lucifer</a></i>), referring to the "<a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_(morning_star)" title="Phosphorus (morning star)">Morning Star</a>", the planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>). The term "<a href="/wiki/Phosphorescence" title="Phosphorescence">phosphorescence</a>", meaning glow after illumination, originally derives from this property of phosphorus, although this word has since been used for a different physical process that produces a glow. The glow of phosphorus itself originates from <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> of the white (but not red) phosphorus — a process now termed <a href="/wiki/Chemiluminescence" title="Chemiluminescence">chemiluminescence</a>. Together with nitrogen, arsenic, antimony, and bismuth, phosphorus is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogen</a>.</p>
<p>Phosphorus is essential for life. <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">Phosphates</a> (compounds containing the phosphate ion, PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>) are a component of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>, which form all cell membranes. Demonstrating the link between phosphorus and life, elemental phosphorus was first isolated from human <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bone_ash" title="Bone ash">bone ash</a> was an important early phosphate source. <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">Phosphate</a> mines contain fossils, especially marine fossils, because phosphate is formed from the deposits of animal remains and excreta. Low phosphate levels are an important limit to growth in some aquatic systems. The vast majority of phosphorus compounds produced are consumed as <a href="/wiki/Fertiliser" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertiliser">fertilisers</a>. Phosphate is needed to replace the phosphorus that plants remove from the soil, and its annual demand is rising nearly twice as fast as the growth of the human population. Other applications include the role of <a href="/wiki/Organophosphorus_compound" title="Organophosphorus compound">organophosphorus compounds</a> in <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nerve_agent" title="Nerve agent">nerve agents</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_phosphorus" title="Allotropes of phosphorus">Allotropes of phosphorus</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg/100px-White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg" width="100" height="93" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg/150px-White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg/200px-White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg 2x" data-file-width="723" data-file-height="675" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_phosphrous_molecule.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
P<sub>4</sub> molecule, as in white phosphorus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Phosphorus exists as several forms (<a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropes</a>) that exhibit strikingly different properties.<sup id="cite_ref-HW85_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HW85-6">[6]</a></sup> The two most common allotropes are <b>white phosphorus</b> and <b>red phosphorus</b>.</p>
<p><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="White_phosphorus_and_related_molecular_forms">White phosphorus and related molecular forms</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phosphorus2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Phosphorus2.jpg/100px-Phosphorus2.jpg" width="100" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Phosphorus2.jpg/150px-Phosphorus2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Phosphorus2.jpg/200px-Phosphorus2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="534" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phosphorus2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
White phosphorus</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png/200px-White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png" width="200" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png/300px-White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png/400px-White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="714" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_phosphorus_glowing_e17.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
White phosphorus exposed to air glows in the darkness</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the perspective of applications and chemical literature, the most important form of elemental phosphorus is <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_phosphorus#White_phosphorus" title="Allotropes of phosphorus">white phosphorus</a>, often abbreviated as <b>WP</b>. It is a soft and waxy solid which consists of tetrahedral <span class="chemf nowrap">P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> molecules, in which each atom is bound to the other three atoms by a single bond. This <span class="chemf nowrap">P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span> tetrahedron is also present in liquid and gaseous phosphorus up to the temperature of 800&#160;°C (1,470&#160;°F) when it starts decomposing to <span class="chemf nowrap">P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> White phosphorus exists in two crystalline forms: α (alpha) and β (beta). At room temperature, the α-form is stable, which is more common and it has cubic crystal structure and at 195.2&#160;K (−78.0&#160;°C), it transforms into β-form, which has hexagonal crystal structure. These forms differ in terms of the relative orientations of the constituent P<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>White phosphorus is the least stable, the most reactive, the most <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a>, the least <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">dense</a>, and the most toxic of the allotropes. White phosphorus gradually changes to red phosphorus. This transformation is accelerated by light and heat, and samples of white phosphorus almost always contain some red phosphorus and accordingly appear yellow. For this reason, white phosphorus that is aged or otherwise impure (e.g. weapons-grade, not lab-grade WP) is also called <b>yellow phosphorus</b>. When exposed to oxygen, white phosphorus glows in the dark with a very faint tinge of green and blue. It is highly <a href="/wiki/Flammable" class="mw-redirect" title="Flammable">flammable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoric</a> (self-igniting) upon contact with air. Owing to its pyrophoricity, white phosphorus is used as an additive in <a href="/wiki/Napalm" title="Napalm">napalm</a>. The odour of combustion of this form has a characteristic garlic smell, and samples are commonly coated with white "<a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentoxide" title="Phosphorus pentoxide">phosphorus pentoxide</a>", which consists of <span class="chemf nowrap">P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
10</span></span> tetrahedra with oxygen inserted between the phosphorus atoms and at their vertices. White phosphorus is insoluble in water but soluble in carbon disulfide.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thermal_Decomposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal Decomposition">Thermolysis</a> (cracking) of P<sub>4</sub> at 1100&#160;kelvin gives <a href="/wiki/Diphosphorus" title="Diphosphorus">diphosphorus</a>, P<sub>2</sub>. This species is not stable as a solid or liquid. The dimeric unit contains a triple bond and is analogous to N<sub>2</sub>. It can also be generated as a transient intermediate in solution by thermolysis of organophosphorus precursor reagents.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> At still higher temperatures, P<sub>2</sub> dissociates into atomic P.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Red_phosphorus">Red phosphorus</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RedPhosphorus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/RedPhosphorus.jpg/125px-RedPhosphorus.jpg" width="125" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/RedPhosphorus.jpg/188px-RedPhosphorus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/RedPhosphorus.jpg/250px-RedPhosphorus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="663" data-file-height="612" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RedPhosphorus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Crystal structure of red phosphorus</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phosphorus1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Phosphorus1.jpg/125px-Phosphorus1.jpg" width="125" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Phosphorus1.jpg/188px-Phosphorus1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Phosphorus1.jpg/250px-Phosphorus1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="667" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phosphorus1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Red phosphorus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Red_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Red phosphorus">Red phosphorus</a> is polymeric in structure. It can be viewed as a derivative of P<sub>4</sub> wherein one P-P bond is broken, and one additional bond is formed with the neighbouring tetrahedron resulting in a chain-like structure. Red phosphorus may be formed by heating white phosphorus to 250&#160;°C (482&#160;°F) or by exposing white phosphorus to sunlight.<sup id="cite_ref-mellor-717_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-717-13">[13]</a></sup> Phosphorus after this treatment is <a href="/wiki/Amorphous" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a>. Upon further heating, this material crystallises. In this sense, red phosphorus is not an allotrope, but rather an intermediate phase between the white and violet phosphorus, and most of its properties have a range of values. For example, freshly prepared, bright red phosphorus is highly reactive and ignites at about 300&#160;°C (572&#160;°F),<sup id="cite_ref-wiberg_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg-14">[14]</a></sup> though it is more stable than white phosphorus, which ignites at about 30&#160;°C (86&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-mellor-721-722_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-721-722-15">[15]</a></sup> After prolonged heating or storage, the color darkens (see infobox images); the resulting product is more stable and does not spontaneously ignite in air.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Violet_phosphorus">Violet phosphorus</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Violet_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Violet phosphorus">Violet phosphorus</a> is a form of phosphorus that can be produced by day-long annealing of red phosphorus above 550&#160;°C. In 1865, <a href="/wiki/Hittorf" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittorf">Hittorf</a> discovered that when phosphorus was recrystallised from molten <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, a red/purple form is obtained. Therefore, this form is sometimes known as "Hittorf's phosphorus" (or violet or α-metallic phosphorus).<sup id="cite_ref-berger_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berger-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_phosphorus">Black phosphorus</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BlackPhosphorus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/BlackPhosphorus.jpg/125px-BlackPhosphorus.jpg" width="125" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/BlackPhosphorus.jpg/188px-BlackPhosphorus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/BlackPhosphorus.jpg/250px-BlackPhosphorus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="425" data-file-height="402" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BlackPhosphorus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Crystal structure of black phosphorus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Black_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Black phosphorus">Black phosphorus</a> is the least reactive allotrope and the thermodynamically stable form below 550&#160;°C (1,022&#160;°F). It is also known as β-metallic phosphorus and has a structure somewhat resembling that of <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Black phosphorus is obtained by heating white phosphorus under high pressures (about 12,000 standard atmospheres or 1.2 gigapascals). It can also be produced at ambient conditions using metal salts, e.g. mercury, as catalysts.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In appearance, properties, and structure, it resembles <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, being black and flaky, a conductor of electricity, and has puckered sheets of linked atoms.<sup id="cite_ref-_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note--21">[21]</a></sup></p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_forms">Other forms</span></h4>
<p>Another form, scarlet phosphorus, is obtained by allowing a solution of white phosphorus in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a> to evaporate in <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>.</p>
<p>Another allotrope is <a href="/wiki/Diphosphorus" title="Diphosphorus">diphosphorus</a>; it contains a phosphorus <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a> as a structural unit and is highly reactive.<sup id="cite_ref-berger_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berger-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_phosphorus" title="Isotopes of phosphorus">Isotopes of phosphorus</a></div>
<p>Twenty-three <a href="/wiki/Isotopes" class="mw-redirect" title="Isotopes">isotopes</a> of phosphorus are known,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> including all possibilities from <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">24<br /></span>P</span> up to <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">46<br /></span>P</span>. Only <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">31<br /></span>P</span> is stable and is therefore present at 100% abundance. The half-integer <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_spin" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear spin">nuclear spin</a> and high abundance of <sup>31</sup>P make <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus-31_NMR" class="mw-redirect" title="Phosphorus-31 NMR">phosphorus-31 NMR</a> spectroscopy a very useful analytical tool in studies of phosphorus-containing samples.</p>
<p>Two <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive isotope">radioactive isotopes</a> of phosphorus have half lives suitable for biological scientific experiments. These are:</p>
<ul>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">32<br /></span>P</span>, a <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta</a>-emitter (1.71 MeV) with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 14.3 days, which is used routinely in life-science laboratories, primarily to produce <a href="/wiki/Radiolabel" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiolabel">radiolabeled</a> DNA and RNA <a href="/wiki/Hybridization_probe" title="Hybridization probe">probes</a>, <i>e.g.</i> for use in <a href="/wiki/Northern_blot" title="Northern blot">Northern blots</a> or <a href="/wiki/Southern_blot" title="Southern blot">Southern blots</a>.</li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">33<br /></span>P</span>, a beta-emitter (0.25&#160;MeV) with a half-life of 25.4 days. It is used in life-science laboratories in applications in which lower energy beta emissions are advantageous such as <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> sequencing.</li>
</ul>
<p>The high energy beta particles from <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">32<br /></span>P</span> penetrate skin and <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">corneas</a> and any <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">32<br /></span>P</span> ingested, inhaled, or absorbed is readily incorporated into bone and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>. For these reasons, <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> in the United States, and similar institutions in other developed countries require personnel working with <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">32<br /></span>P</span> to wear lab coats, disposable gloves, and safety glasses or goggles to protect the eyes, and avoid working directly over open containers. <a href="/wiki/Biomonitoring" title="Biomonitoring">Monitoring</a> personal, clothing, and surface contamination is also required. <a href="/wiki/Radiation_protection" title="Radiation protection">Shielding</a> requires special consideration. The high energy of the beta particles gives rise to secondary emission of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> via <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">Bremsstrahlung</a> (braking radiation) in dense shielding materials such as lead. Therefore, the radiation must be shielded with low density materials such as acrylic or other plastic, water, or (when transparency is not required), even wood.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote selfreference">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Phosphate_minerals" title="Category:Phosphate minerals">Category:Phosphate minerals</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minerals_.28phosphate_rock.29">Minerals (phosphate rock)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru,_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_(10729889683).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru%2C_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_%2810729889683%29.jpg/200px-The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru%2C_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_%2810729889683%29.jpg" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru%2C_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_%2810729889683%29.jpg/300px-The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru%2C_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_%2810729889683%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru%2C_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_%2810729889683%29.jpg/400px-The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru%2C_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_%2810729889683%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_site_of_secondary_mining_of_Phosphate_rock_in_Nauru,_2007._Photo-_Lorrie_Graham_(10729889683).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mining of phosphate rock in <a href="/wiki/Nauru" title="Nauru">Nauru</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific ocean">Pacific Ocean</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At 0.099%, phosphorus is the most abundant <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogen</a> in the Earth's crust but it is not found free in nature; it is widely distributed in many <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, mainly phosphates.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Inorganic <a href="/wiki/Phosphate_rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Phosphate rock">phosphate rock</a>, which is partially made of <a href="/wiki/Apatite" title="Apatite">apatite</a> (an impure tri-<a href="/wiki/Calcium_phosphate" title="Calcium phosphate">calcium phosphate</a> mineral), is today the chief commercial source of this element. According to the <a href="/wiki/US_Geological_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="US Geological Survey">US Geological Survey (USGS)</a>, about 50 percent of the global phosphorus reserves are in the Arab nations.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-25">[25]</a></sup> Large deposits of apatite are located in <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-MJ_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MJ-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albright_and_Wilson" title="Albright and Wilson">Albright and Wilson</a> in the UK and their <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls" title="Niagara Falls">Niagara Falls</a> plant, for instance, were using phosphate rock in the 1890s and 1900s from Tennessee, Florida, and the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Eles_du_Conn%C3%A9table" title="Îles du Connétable">Îles du Connétable</a> (<a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a> island sources of phosphate); by 1950 they were using phosphate rock mainly from Tennessee and North Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-threlfall-51_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threlfall-51-28">[28]</a></sup> In the early 1990s Albright and Wilson's purified wet phosphoric acid business was being adversely affected by phosphate rock sales by China and the entry of their long-standing Moroccan phosphate suppliers into the purified wet phosphoric acid business.<sup id="cite_ref-podger-287-298_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-podger-287-298-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg/150px-DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg" width="150" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg/225px-DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg/300px-DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="558" data-file-height="730" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN5766-guano-glantz_crop_b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">Guano</a> mining in the Central <a href="/wiki/Chincha_Islands" title="Chincha Islands">Chincha Islands</a>, ca. 1860.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2012, the USGS estimated 71 billion tons of world reserves, where reserve figures refer to the amount assumed recoverable at current market prices; 0.19 billion tons were mined in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Critical to contemporary agriculture, its annual demand is rising nearly twice as fast as the growth of the human population.<sup id="cite_ref-MJ_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MJ-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent reports suggest that production of phosphorus may have peaked, leading to the possibility of global shortages by 2040.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 2007, at the rate of consumption, the supply of phosphorus was estimated to run out in 345 years.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> However, some scientists now believe that a "<a href="/wiki/Peak_phosphorus" title="Peak phosphorus">peak phosphorus</a>" will occur in 30 years and that "At current rates, reserves will be depleted in the next 50 to 100 years."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Cofounder of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>-based investment firm and environmental foundation <a href="/wiki/Grantham" title="Grantham">Jeremy Grantham</a> wrote in <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)"><i>Nature</i></a> in November 2012, that consumption of the element "must be drastically reduced in the next 20-40 years or we will begin to starve."<sup id="cite_ref-MJ_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MJ-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> According to N.N. Greenwood and A. Earnshaw, authors of the textbook, <i>Chemistry of the Elements,</i> however, phosphorus comprises about 0.1% by mass of the average rock, and consequently the Earth's supply is vast, although dilute.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urine">Urine</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reuse_of_excreta" title="Reuse of excreta">Reuse of excreta</a></div>
<p>Urine contains most (94% according to Wolgast<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>) of the <a href="/wiki/NPK" class="mw-redirect" title="NPK">NPK</a> nutrients excreted by the human body. The more general limitations to using urine as fertiliser depend mainly on the potential for buildup of excess nitrogen (due to the high ratio of that macronutrient), and inorganic <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>, which are also part of the wastes excreted by the <a href="/wiki/Renal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Renal system">renal system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The degree to which these factors impact the effectiveness depends on the term of use, salinity tolerance of the plant, soil composition, addition of other fertilising compounds, and quantity of rainfall or other irrigation.</p>
<p>Urine typically contributes 70% of the <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and more than half the phosphorus and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> found in urban waste water flows, while making up less than 1% of the overall volume. Thus far, source separation, or <a href="/wiki/Urine_diversion" title="Urine diversion">urine diversion</a> and on-site treatment, has been implemented in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> among other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Urine management" is a relatively new way to view closing the cycle of agricultural nutrient flows and reducing sewage treatment costs and ecological consequences such as <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> resulting from the influx of nutrient rich effluent into aquatic or marine ecosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Proponents of urine as a natural source of agricultural fertiliser claim the risks to be negligible or acceptable.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_organic_sources">Other organic sources</span></h3>
<p>Historically-important but limited commercial sources were organic, such as bone ash and (in the latter 19th century) <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supernovae_remnants">Supernovae remnants</span></h3>
<p>In 2013, astronomers detected phosphorus in <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_A" title="Cassiopeia A">Cassiopeia A</a>, which confirmed that this element is produced in <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a> as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova nucleosynthesis</a>. The phosphorus-to-<a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> ratio in material from the <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnant</a> could be up to 100 times higher than in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> in general.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">Phosphoric acid</a></div>
<p>Most production of phosphorus-bearing material is for agriculture fertilisers. For this purpose, phosphate minerals are converted to phosphoric acid. It follows two distinct chemical routes, the main one being treatment of phosphate minerals with sulfuric acid. The other process utilises white phosphorus, which may be produced by reaction and distillation from very low grade phosphate sources. The white phosphorus is then oxidised to phosphoric acid and subsequently neutralised with base to give phosphate salts. Phosphoric acid produced from white phosphorus is relatively pure and is the main route for the production of phosphates for all purposes, including detergent production.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elemental_phosphorus">Elemental phosphorus</span></h3>
<p>Presently, about 1,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a> (910,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">t</a>) of elemental phosphorus is produced annually. Calcium phosphate (phosphate rock), mostly mined in Florida and North Africa, can be heated to 1,200–1,500&#160;°C with sand, which is mostly <span class="chemf nowrap">SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> (refined coal) to produce vaporised <span class="chemf nowrap">P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span>. The product is subsequently condensed into a white powder under water to prevent oxidation by air. Even under water, <a href="/wiki/White_phosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="White phosphorus">white phosphorus</a> is slowly converted to the more stable red phosphorus <a href="/wiki/Allotrope" class="mw-redirect" title="Allotrope">allotrope</a>. The chemical equation for this process when starting with fluoroapatite, a common phosphate mineral, is:</p>
<dl>
<dd>4 Ca<sub>5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F + 18 SiO<sub>2</sub> + 30 C → 3 P<sub>4</sub> + 30 CO + 18 CaSiO<sub>3</sub> + 2 CaF<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p>Side products from this process include ferrophosphorus, a crude form of Fe<sub>2</sub>P, resulting from iron impurities in the mineral precursors. The silicate <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a> is a useful construction material. The fluoride is sometimes recovered for use in <a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation" title="Water fluoridation">water fluoridation</a>. More problematic is a "mud" containing significant amounts of white phosphorus. Production of white phosphorus is conducted in large facilities in part because it is energy intensive. The white phosphorus is transported in molten form. Some major accidents have occurred during transportation; train derailments at <a href="/wiki/Brownstone" title="Brownstone">Brownston, Nebraska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miamisburg,_Ohio" title="Miamisburg, Ohio">Miamisburg, Ohio</a> led to large fires. The worst incident in recent times was an environmental contamination in 1968 when the sea was polluted from spillage and/or inadequately treated sewage from a white phosphorus plant at <a href="/wiki/Placentia_Bay,_Newfoundland" class="mw-redirect" title="Placentia Bay, Newfoundland">Placentia Bay, Newfoundland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another process by which elemental phosphorus is extracted includes applying at high temperatures (1500&#160;°C):<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>2 Ca<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 6 SiO<sub>2</sub> + 10 C → 6 CaSiO<sub>3</sub> + 10 CO + P<sub>4</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p>Historically, before the development of mineral-based extractions, white phosphorus was isolated on an industrial scale from bone ash.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In this process, the <a href="/wiki/Tricalcium_phosphate" title="Tricalcium phosphate">tricalcium phosphate</a> in bone ash is converted to <a href="/wiki/Monocalcium_phosphate" title="Monocalcium phosphate">monocalcium phosphate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Ca<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> → Ca(H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 2 CaSO<sub>4</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monocalcium_phosphate" title="Monocalcium phosphate">Monocalcium phosphate</a> is then dehydrated to the corresponding metaphosphate:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Ca(H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> → Ca(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
</dl>
<p>When ignited to a white heat with <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, calcium metaphosphate yields two-thirds of its weight of white phosphorus while one-third of the phosphorus remains in the residue as calcium orthophosphate:</p>
<dl>
<dd>3 Ca(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 10 C → Ca<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 10 CO + P<sub>4</sub></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote selfreference">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Phosphorus_compounds" title="Category:Phosphorus compounds">Category:Phosphorus compounds</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxoacids_of_phosphorus">Oxoacids of phosphorus</span></h3>
<p>Phosphorous oxoacids are extensive, often commercially important, and sometimes structurally complicated. They all have acidic protons bound to oxygen atoms, some have nonacidic protons that are bonded directly to phosphorus and some contain phosphorus - phosphorus bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup> Although many oxoacids of phosphorus are formed, only nine are important, and three of them, <a href="/wiki/Hypophosphorous_acid" title="Hypophosphorous acid">hypophosphorous acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorous_acid" title="Phosphorous acid">phosphorous acid</a>, and phosphoric acid, are particularly important.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphorus.28V.29_compounds">Phosphorus(V) compounds</span></h3>
<p>The most prevalent compounds of phosphorus are derivatives of phosphate (PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>), a tetrahedral anion.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Phosphate is the conjugate base of phosphoric acid, which is produced on a massive scale for use in fertilisers. Being triprotic, phosphoric acid converts stepwise to three conjugate bases:</p>
<dl>
<dd>H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>= 7.25×10<sup>−3</sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> + HPO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; <i>K</i><sub>a2</sub>= 6.31×10<sup>−8</sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>HPO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> + &#160;PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; <i>K</i><sub>a3</sub>= 3.98×10<sup>−13</sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Phosphate exhibits the tendency to form chains and rings with P-O-P bonds. Many polyphosphates are known, including <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>. Polyphosphates arise by dehydration of hydrogen phosphates such as HPO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> and H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>. For example, the industrially important trisodium triphosphate (also known as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_tripolyphosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium tripolyphosphate">sodium tripolyphosphate</a>, STPP) is produced industrially on by the megatonne by this <a href="/wiki/Condensation_reaction" title="Condensation reaction">condensation reaction</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>2 Na<sub>2</sub>[(HO)PO<sub>3</sub>] + Na[(HO)<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub>] → Na<sub>5</sub>[O<sub>3</sub>P-O-P(O)<sub>2</sub>-O-PO<sub>3</sub>] + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentoxide" title="Phosphorus pentoxide">Phosphorus pentoxide</a> (P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub>) is the <a href="/wiki/Acid_anhydride" class="mw-redirect" title="Acid anhydride">acid anhydride</a> of phosphoric acid, but several intermediates between the two are known. This waxy white solid reacts vigorously with water.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png/220px-Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png/330px-Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png/440px-Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1097" data-file-height="1100" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phosphorus-pentoxide-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The tetrahedral structure of P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub> and P<sub>4</sub>S<sub>10</sub>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With metal <a href="/wiki/Cation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cation">cations</a>, phosphate forms a variety of salts. These solids are polymeric, featuring P-O-M linkages. When the metal cation has a charge of 2+ or 3+, the salts are generally insoluble, hence they exist as common minerals. Many phosphate salts are derived from hydrogen phosphate (HPO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup>).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentachloride" title="Phosphorus pentachloride">PCl<sub>5</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentafluoride" title="Phosphorus pentafluoride">PF<sub>5</sub></a> are common compounds. PF<sub>5</sub> is a colourless gas and the molecules have <a href="/wiki/Trigonal_bipyramid" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonal bipyramid">trigonal bypramidal</a> geometry. PCl<sub>5</sub> is a colourless solid which has an ionic formulation of PCl<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> PCl<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, but adopts the <a href="/wiki/Trigonal_bipyramid" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonal bipyramid">trigonal bypramidal</a> geometry when molten or in the vapour phase.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentabromide" title="Phosphorus pentabromide">PBr<sub>5</sub></a> is an unstable solid formulated as PBr<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>Br<sup>−</sup>and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentaiodide" title="Phosphorus pentaiodide">PI<sub>5</sub></a> is not known.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup> The pentachloride and pentafluoride are <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acids</a>. With fluoride, PF<sub>5</sub> forms PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, an <a href="/wiki/Anion" class="mw-redirect" title="Anion">anion</a> that is <a href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" class="mw-redirect" title="Isoelectronic">isoelectronic</a> with SF<sub>6</sub>. The most important oxyhalide is <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_oxychloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Phosphorus oxychloride">phosphorus oxychloride</a>, (POCl<sub>3</sub>), which is approximately tetrahedral.</p>
<p>Before extensive computer calculations were feasible, it was thought that bonding in phosphorus(V) compounds involved <i>d</i> orbitals. Computer modeling of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital_theory" title="Molecular orbital theory">molecular orbital theory</a> indicates that this bonding involves only s- and p-orbitals.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrides">Nitrides</span></h3>
<p>The PN molecule is considered unstable, but is a product of crystalline <a href="/wiki/Triphosphorus_pentanitride" title="Triphosphorus pentanitride">Phosphorus nitride</a> decomposition at 1100 K. Similarly, H<sub>2</sub>PN is considered unstable, and phosphorus nitride halogens like F<sub>2</sub>PN, Cl<sub>2</sub>PN, Br<sub>2</sub>PN, and I<sub>2</sub>PN oligomerise into cyclic <a href="/wiki/Polyphosphazene" title="Polyphosphazene">Polyphosphazenes</a>. For example, compounds of the formula (PNCl<sub>2</sub>)<sub>n</sub> exist mainly as rings such as the <a href="/wiki/Trimer_(chemistry)" title="Trimer (chemistry)">trimer</a> <a href="/wiki/Hexachlorophosphazene" title="Hexachlorophosphazene">hexachlorophosphazene</a>. The phosphazenes arise by treatment of phosphorus pentachloride with ammonium chloride:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>PCl<sub>5</sub> + NH<sub>4</sub>Cl → 1/<i>n</i> (NPCl<sub>2</sub>)<sub><i>n</i></sub> + 4 HCl</p>
</blockquote>
<p>When the chloride groups are replaced by <a href="/wiki/Alkoxide" title="Alkoxide">alkoxide</a> (RO<sup>−</sup>), a family of polymers is produced with potentially useful properties.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfides">Sulfides</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_sulfide" title="Phosphorus sulfide">phosphorus sulfide</a></div>
<p>Phosphorus forms a wide range of sulfides, where the phosphorus can be in P(V), P(III) or other oxidation states. The most famous is the three-fold symmetric P<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> which is used in strike-anywhere matches. P<sub>4</sub>S<sub>10</sub> and P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub> have analogous structures.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphorus.28III.29_compounds">Phosphorus(III) compounds</span></h3>
<p>All four symmetrical trihalides are well known: gaseous <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_trifluoride" title="Phosphorus trifluoride">PF<sub>3</sub></a>, the yellowish liquids <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_trichloride" title="Phosphorus trichloride">PCl<sub>3</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_tribromide" title="Phosphorus tribromide">PBr<sub>3</sub></a>, and the solid <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_triiodide" title="Phosphorus triiodide">PI<sub>3</sub></a>. These materials are moisture sensitive, hydrolysing to give <a href="/wiki/Phosphorous_acid" title="Phosphorous acid">phosphorous acid</a>. The trichloride, a common reagent, is produced by chlorination of white phosphorus:</p>
<dl>
<dd>P<sub>4</sub> + 6 Cl<sub>2</sub> → 4 PCl<sub>3</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p>The trifluoride is produced from the trichloride by halide exchange. PF<sub>3</sub> is toxic because it binds to <a href="/wiki/Haemoglobin" class="mw-redirect" title="Haemoglobin">haemoglobin</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_trioxide" title="Phosphorus trioxide">Phosphorus(III) oxide</a>, P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>6</sub> (also called tetraphosphorus hexoxide) is the anhydride of P(OH)<sub>3</sub>, the minor tautomer of phosphorous acid. The structure of P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>6</sub> is like that of P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub> without the terminal oxide groups.</p>
<p>Mixed oxyhalides and oxyhydrides of phosphorus(III) are almost unknown.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organophosphorus_compounds">Organophosphorus compounds</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organophosphorus_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Organophosphorus compounds">organophosphorus compounds</a></div>
<p>Compounds with P-C and P-O-C bonds are often classified as organophosphorus compounds. They are widely used commercially. The PCl<sub>3</sub> serves as a source of P<sup>3+</sup> in routes to organophosphorus(III) compounds. For example, it is the precursor to <a href="/wiki/Triphenylphosphine" title="Triphenylphosphine">triphenylphosphine</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>PCl<sub>3</sub> + 6 Na + 3 C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>Cl → P(C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub> + 6 NaCl</dd>
</dl>
<p>Treatment of phosphorus trihalides with alcohols and <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenols</a> gives phosphites, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Triphenylphosphite" class="mw-redirect" title="Triphenylphosphite">triphenylphosphite</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>PCl<sub>3</sub> + 3 C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH → P(OC<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub> + 3 HCl</dd>
</dl>
<p>Similar reactions occur for <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_oxychloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Phosphorus oxychloride">phosphorus oxychloride</a>, affording <a href="/wiki/Triphenylphosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Triphenylphosphate">triphenylphosphate</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>OPCl<sub>3</sub> + 3 C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH → OP(OC<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>3</sub> + 3 HCl</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphorus.28I.29_and_phosphorus.28II.29_compounds">Phosphorus(I) and phosphorus(II) compounds</span></h3>
<p>These compounds generally feature P-P bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup> Examples include catenated derivatives of phosphine and organophosphines. Compounds containing P=P double bonds have also been observed, although they are rare.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:YoshifujiR2P2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/YoshifujiR2P2.png/220px-YoshifujiR2P2.png" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/YoshifujiR2P2.png/330px-YoshifujiR2P2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/YoshifujiR2P2.png/440px-YoshifujiR2P2.png 2x" data-file-width="654" data-file-height="553" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:YoshifujiR2P2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A stable <a href="/wiki/Diphosphene" title="Diphosphene">diphosphene</a>, a derivative of phosphorus(I).</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphines">Phosphines</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">Phosphine</a> (PH<sub>3</sub>) and its organic derivatives (PR<sub>3</sub>) are structural analogues with ammonia (NH<sub>3</sub>) but the bond angles at phosphorus are closer to 90° for phosphine and its organic derivatives. It is an ill-smelling, toxic compound. Phosphorus has an oxidation number of -3 in phosphine. Phosphine is produced by hydrolysis of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_phosphide" title="Calcium phosphide">calcium phosphide</a>, Ca<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>. Unlike ammonia, phosphine is oxidised by air. Phosphine is also far less basic than ammonia. Other phophines are known which contain chains of up to nine phosphorus atoms and have the formula P<sub>n</sub>H<sub>n+2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup> The highly flammable gas <a href="/wiki/Diphosphine" class="mw-redirect" title="Diphosphine">diphosphine</a> (P<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>) is an analogue of <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphides">Phosphides</span></h3>
<p>Phosphides arise by reaction of metals with red phosphorus. The alkali metals (group 1) and alkaline earth metals can form ionic compounds containing the <a href="/wiki/Phosphide" title="Phosphide">phosphide</a> ion, P<sup>3−</sup>. These compounds react with water to form <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a>. Other phosphides, for example Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>7</sub>, are known for these reactive metals. With the transition metals as well as the monophosphides there are metal rich phosphides, which are generally hard refractory compounds with a metallic lustre, and phosphorus rich phosphides which are less stable and include semiconductors.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Schreibersite" title="Schreibersite">Schreibersite</a> is a naturally occurring metal rich phosphide found in meteorites. The structures of the metal rich and phosphorus rich phosphides can be structurally complex.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_and_etymology">Spelling and etymology</span></h2>
<p>The name <i>Phosphorus</i> in Ancient Greece was the name for the planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> words (φῶς = light, φέρω = carry), which roughly translates as light-bringer or light carrier.<sup id="cite_ref-mellor-717_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-717-13">[13]</a></sup> (In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a> and tradition, Augerinus (Αυγερινός = morning star, still in use today), Hesperus or Hesperinus (΄Εσπερος or Εσπερινός or Αποσπερίτης = evening star, still in use today) and Eosphorus (Εωσφόρος = dawnbearer, not in use for the planet after Christianity) are close homologues, and also associated with Phosphorus-the-planet).</p>
<p>According to the Oxford English Dictionary, the correct spelling of the element is <b>phosphorus</b>. The word <b>phosphorous</b> is the adjectival form of the P<sup>3+</sup> valence: so, just as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> forms sulfur<b>ous</b> and sulfur<b>ic</b> compounds, phosphor<b>us</b> forms phosphor<b>ous</b> compounds (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Phosphorous_acid" title="Phosphorous acid">phosphorous acid</a>) and P<sup>5+</sup> valence phosphor<b>ic</b> compounds (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acids_and_phosphates" title="Phosphoric acids and phosphates">phosphoric acids and phosphates</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_discovery">History and discovery</span></h2>
<p>Phosphorus was the 13th element to be discovered. For this reason, and also due to its use in explosives, poisons and nerve agents, it is sometimes referred to as "the Devil's element".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> It was the first element to be discovered that was not known since ancient times.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The discovery of phosphorus is credited to the German alchemist <a href="/wiki/Hennig_Brand" title="Hennig Brand">Hennig Brand</a> in 1669, although other chemists might have discovered phosphorus around the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-disc_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-disc-52">[52]</a></sup> Brand experimented with <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>, which contains considerable quantities of dissolved phosphates from normal metabolism.<sup id="cite_ref-mellor-717_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-717-13">[13]</a></sup> Working in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, Brand attempted to create the fabled <a href="/wiki/Philosopher%27s_stone" title="Philosopher's stone">philosopher's stone</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> of some <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salts</a> by evaporating urine, and in the process produced a white material that glowed in the dark and burned brilliantly. It was named <i>phosphorus mirabilis</i> ("miraculous bearer of light").<sup id="cite_ref-schmundt_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmundt-53">[53]</a></sup> His process originally involved letting urine stand for days until it gave off a terrible smell. Then he boiled it down to a paste, heated this paste to a high temperature, and led the vapours through water, where he hoped they would condense to gold. Instead, he obtained a white, waxy substance that glowed in the dark. Brand had discovered phosphorus. We now know that Brand produced ammonium sodium hydrogen phosphate, <span class="chemf nowrap">(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span>)NaHPO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span>. While the quantities were essentially correct (it took about 1,100 litres [290&#160;US&#160;gal] of urine to make about 60&#160;g of phosphorus), it was unnecessary to allow the urine to rot. Later scientists discovered that fresh urine yielded the same amount of phosphorus.<sup id="cite_ref-sommers_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sommers-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_boyle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Robert_boyle.jpg/170px-Robert_boyle.jpg" width="170" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Robert_boyle.jpg/255px-Robert_boyle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Robert_boyle.jpg/340px-Robert_boyle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_boyle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Robert Boyle</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Brand at first tried to keep the method secret,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> but later sold the recipe for 200 thalers to D Krafft from Dresden,<sup id="cite_ref-mellor-717_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-717-13">[13]</a></sup> who could now make it as well, and toured much of Europe with it, including England, where he met with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>. The secret that it was made from urine leaked out and first <a href="/wiki/Johann_von_L%C3%B6wenstern-Kunckel" title="Johann von Löwenstern-Kunckel">Johann Kunckel</a> (1630–1703) in Sweden (1678) and later Boyle in London (1680) also managed to make phosphorus, possibly with the aid of his assistant, <a href="/wiki/Ambrose_Godfrey" title="Ambrose Godfrey">Ambrose Godfrey-Hanckwitz</a>, who later made a business of the manufacture of phosphorus. Boyle states that Krafft gave him no information as to the preparation of phosphorus other than that it was derived from "somewhat that belonged to the body of man". This gave Boyle a valuable clue, so that he, too, managed to make phosphorus, and published the method of its manufacture.<sup id="cite_ref-mellor-717_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-717-13">[13]</a></sup> Later he improved Brand's process by using sand in the reaction (still using urine as base material),</p>
<dl>
<dd>4 <span class="chemf nowrap">NaPO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> + 10 C → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span> + 10 CO + <span class="chemf nowrap">P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Robert Boyle was the first to use phosphorus to ignite sulfur-tipped wooden splints, forerunners of our modern matches, in 1680.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1769 <a href="/wiki/Johan_Gottlieb_Gahn" title="Johan Gottlieb Gahn">Johan Gottlieb Gahn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> showed that calcium phosphate (<span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>(PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
4</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>) is found in bones, and they obtained elemental phosphorus from bone ash. <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> recognised phosphorus as an element in 1777.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Bone ash was the major source of phosphorus until the 1840s. The method started by roasting bones, then employed the use of clay <a href="/wiki/Retorts" class="mw-redirect" title="Retorts">retorts</a> encased in a very hot brick furnace to distill out the highly toxic elemental phosphorus product.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Alternately, precipitated phosphates could be made from ground-up bones that had been de-greased and treated with strong acids. White phosphorus could then be made by heating the precipitated phosphates, mixed with ground coal or <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> in an iron pot, and distilling off phosphorus vapour in a <a href="/wiki/Retort" title="Retort">retort</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-threlfall-v_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threlfall-v-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">Carbon monoxide</a> and other flammable gases produced during the reduction process were burnt off in a flare stack.</p>
<p>In the 1840s, world phosphate production turned to the mining of tropical island deposits formed from bird and bat <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Guano_Islands_Act" title="Guano Islands Act">Guano Islands Act</a>). These became an important source of phosphates for fertiliser in the latter half of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phosphate_rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Phosphate rock">Phosphate rock</a>, a mineral containing calcium phosphate, was first used in 1850 to make phosphorus, and following the introduction of the electric arc furnace in 1890, elemental phosphorus production switched from the bone-ash heating, to electric arc production from phosphate rock. After the depletion of world guano sources about the same time, mineral phosphates became the major source of phosphate fertiliser production. Phosphate rock production greatly increased after World War II, and remains the primary global source of phosphorus and phosphorus chemicals today. See the article on <a href="/wiki/Peak_phosphorus" title="Peak phosphorus">peak phosphorus</a> for more information on the history and present state of phosphate mining. Phosphate rock remains a feedstock in the fertiliser industry, where it is treated with sulfuric acid to produce various "<a href="/wiki/Superphosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Superphosphate">superphosphate</a>" fertiliser products.</p>
<p>White phosphorus was first made commercially in the 19th century for the <a href="/wiki/Match" title="Match">match</a> industry. This used bone ash for a phosphate source, as described above. The bone-ash process became obsolete when the <a href="/wiki/Submerged-arc_furnace_for_phosphorus_production" title="Submerged-arc furnace for phosphorus production">submerged-arc furnace for phosphorus production</a> was introduced to reduce phosphate rock.<sup id="cite_ref-threlfall-vii_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threlfall-vii-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mellor-718-720_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-718-720-62">[62]</a></sup> The electric furnace method allowed production to increase to the point where phosphorus could be used in weapons of war.<sup id="cite_ref-shockinghistory_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shockinghistory-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-threlfall-xi_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threlfall-xi-64">[64]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> it was used in incendiaries, <a href="/wiki/Smoke_screen" title="Smoke screen">smoke screens</a> and tracer bullets.<sup id="cite_ref-threlfall-xi_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threlfall-xi-64">[64]</a></sup> A special incendiary bullet was developed to shoot at <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>-filled <a href="/wiki/Zeppelin" title="Zeppelin">Zeppelins</a> over Britain (hydrogen being highly <a href="/wiki/Flammable" class="mw-redirect" title="Flammable">flammable</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-threlfall-xi_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threlfall-xi-64">[64]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molotov_cocktail" title="Molotov cocktail">Molotov cocktails</a> made of phosphorus dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Petrol" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrol">petrol</a> were distributed in Britain to specially selected civilians within the British resistance operation, for defence; and phosphorus incendiary bombs were used in war on a large scale. Burning phosphorus is difficult to extinguish and if it splashes onto human skin it has horrific effects.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early matches used white phosphorus in their composition, which was dangerous due to its toxicity. Murders, suicides and accidental <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisonings</a> resulted from its use. (An apocryphal tale tells of a woman attempting to murder her husband with white phosphorus in his food, which was detected by the stew's giving off luminous steam).<sup id="cite_ref-shockinghistory_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shockinghistory-63">[63]</a></sup> In addition, exposure to the vapours gave match workers a severe <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a> of the bones of the jaw, the infamous "<a href="/wiki/Phossy_jaw" title="Phossy jaw">phossy jaw</a>". When a safe process for manufacturing red phosphorus was discovered, with its far lower flammability and toxicity, laws were enacted, under the <a href="/wiki/Berne_Convention_(1906)" title="Berne Convention (1906)">Berne Convention (1906)</a>, requiring its adoption as a safer alternative for match manufacture.<sup id="cite_ref-phbook_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phbook-65">[65]</a></sup> The toxicity of white phosphorus led to discontinuation of its use in matches.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The Allies used phosphorus <a href="/wiki/Incendiary_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Incendiary bomb">incendiary bombs</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> to destroy Hamburg, the place where the "miraculous bearer of light" was first discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-schmundt_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmundt-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glow">Glow</span></h3>
<p>It was known from early times that the green glow emanating from white phosphorus would persist for a time in a stoppered jar, but then cease. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> in the 1680s ascribed it to "debilitation" of the air; in fact, it is oxygen being consumed. By the 18th century, it was known that in pure oxygen, phosphorus does not glow at all;<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> there is only a range of <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a> at which it does. Heat can be applied to drive the reaction at higher pressures.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1974, the glow was explained by R. J. van Zee and A. U. Khan.<sup id="cite_ref-shockinghistory_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shockinghistory-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> A reaction with oxygen takes place at the surface of the solid (or liquid) phosphorus, forming the short-lived molecules HPO and <span class="chemf nowrap">P<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> that both emit visible light. The reaction is slow and only very little of the intermediates are required to produce the luminescence, hence the extended time the glow continues in a stoppered jar.</p>
<p>Since that time, <i><a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphors</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Phosphorescence" title="Phosphorescence">phosphorescence</a></i> were used loosely to describe substances that shine in the dark without burning. Although the term <a href="/wiki/Phosphorescence" title="Phosphorescence">phosphorescence</a> is derived from phosphorus, the reaction that gives phosphorus its glow is properly called <a href="/wiki/Chemiluminescence" title="Chemiluminescence">chemiluminescence</a> (glowing due to a cold chemical reaction), not phosphorescence (re-emitting light that previously fell onto a substance and excited it).<sup id="cite_ref-sommers_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sommers-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertiliser">Fertiliser</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fertiliser" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertiliser">Fertiliser</a></div>
<p>Phosphorus is an essential plant nutrient (often the limiting nutrient), and the bulk of all phosphorus production is in concentrated phosphoric acids for <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> <a href="/wiki/Fertiliser" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertiliser">fertiliser</a>s, containing as much as 70% to 75% P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Its annual demand is rising nearly twice as fast as the growth of the human population. That led to large increase in <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> (PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>) production in the second half of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-MJ_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MJ-26">[26]</a></sup> Artificial phosphate fertilisation is necessary because phosphorus is essential to all life organisms, natural phosphorus-bearing compounds are mostly insoluble and inaccessible to plants, and the natural cycle of phosphorus is very slow. Fertiliser is often in the form of superphosphate of lime, a mixture of calcium dihydrogen phosphate (Ca(H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>), and calcium sulfate dihydrate (CaSO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O) produced reacting sulfuric acid and water with calcium phosphate.</p>
<p>Processing phosphate minerals with sulfuric acid for obtaining fertiliser is so important to the global economy that this is the primary industrial market for <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> and the greatest industrial use of elemental <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>

<th>Use</th>
</tr>
